The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the
Jewish Progressive Society is organized to encourage
thrift and provide a safe, convenient medium where¬
by members can invest and accumulate savings. The
Credit Union carries life savings insurance of $2,000
and loan protection insurance without any direct cost
to the members. Last year the Board declared a 5%
dividend on savings and 10% rebate of interest to
borrowing members.
Ten Commandments of our Credit Union: The
Member is the most important person in our Credit
Union. The Member is not an outsider, he is an owner
of our Credit Union. The Member is not an interrup¬
tion of our work, he is the purpose of it. The Member
does us a favor when he calls — We are not doing
him a favor when we serve him. The Member is not
a cold statistic — He is a human being with feelings
and emotions like our own. The Member is not some¬
one to argue or match wits with. The Member is a
person who brings us his wants -— it is our job to
fill these wants. The Member is deserving of the most
courteous and attentive treatment we can give him.
The Member is not dependent o nus, we are dependent
on him. The Member is the life blood of this Credit
Union.
The year’s activities climax with the annual dinner
meeting at the Center. Anyone who wishes to join is
extended a cordial invitation.

The oldest active veteran organization in the
United States is the patriotic voice of American Jewry
in affiliation with all major patriotic organizations, vis¬
iting hospitalized veterans, decorating the graves of
Jewish soldiers of all wars. As members of the Com¬
munity Council we participate in all movements in
the interest of the Jewish community.

OFFICERS
President Roland Mizrahi
1st Vice-President Jeff Barnett
2nd Vice-President Nathan Gendzier
3rd Vice-President Jeff Epstein
Recording Vice-President Eileen Raskin
Corresponding Secretary Robin Jawitz
Treasurer Stephen Presser
Dear Friends:
This past summer when I attended the Leadership
Training Institute held at Camp Blue Star at Hender¬
sonville, N.C., the first thing they taught me was the
aims of United Synagogue Youth. They were: to
offer opportunities to the Jewish youth to continue to
strengthen his identification with Judaism and with
the Synagogue as the central agency of Jewish life
in America, and to give Jewish youth an awareness of
the essential harmony between the ideals and tradi¬
tions of Judaism and American Democracy.
I feel that this past year the Charles Palot Club
has strived to reach these goals. The Charles Palot
Club got off to a good start as we headed off to
Goldhead State Park for a day of pure enjoyment.
We followed this up by some enthusiastic meetings
including Jewish Jeopardy and a Swim Party. Our
programs also included a variety of relevant political
and religious topics such as an Aggression Lab, “The
Relevant Yom Kippur”, a dinner-discussion meeting
on inter-faith dating, and a discussion on Soviet Jewry
led by Rabbi David Gaffney topped off by a phone
call to a Soviet Jew in Moscow.
Officers shown with their leaders,
Rhoda and Arthur Goldstein
I would like to thank my advisors, Rhoda and
Arthur Goldstein and Larry Browdy, as well as my
fellow officers for the great assistance they gave in
helping mold our club into one of the most respected
clubs in the south. I would also like to thank Mrs.
Blanche Slott for the encouragement and guidance
throughout the year.
Best wishes for the future of the Charles Palot
Club, and may the members continue to strengthen
their identification with Judaism and with the syna¬
gogue as a central agency of Jewish life.
Shalom,
Michael Shorstein

OFFICERS
President Jay Plotkin
Vice-President Wendy Roth
Secretary Margie Cohen
Treasurer Shana Safer
Membership Chairman Heidi Crystal
Program Chairman Lawrence Ansbacher
Kadima is the youngest USY group and limits its
membership to seventh graders only. Activities are
carefully geared toward this group with a liberal
amount of social and fellowship blended into a mean¬
ingful program of Conservative Judaism.
Kadima’s last year’s activities included roller
skating, business meetings, participation in the Purim
Carnival and a dance at the Plantation Apartments
which introduced the sixth graders to Kadima.
An innovation this summer was a special leader¬
ship training conference for Kadima only in North
Carolina which was attended by the following Jack¬
sonville delegates, Sharon and Marie Saraga, and
Eileen Raskin.
Advisors for this year are Mrs. Lewis Ansbacher,
Lauren Schemer and Richard Ansbacher. They and
the entire enthusiastic group invite all seventh graders
to joint them for a wonderful year in Kadima.
103

===== PAGE 14 =====
a
transportation
The Fleet of Buses. The Drivers, from left: Spencer Everett, Morris
Montford, George B. Starks and Willie Hay.
The Transportation Committee of the Center is very active in developing
a system of routes as well as providing the necessary vehicles.
We have three 66 passenger buses as well as a 12 passenger mini-bus.
Our staff of drivers is headed by Morris Montford, a twenty year plus
veteran with an enviable record. He teams with Willie Hay, George Starks
and Spencer Everett to transport our children from their homes and schools
to classes, activities and services at the Center.
Our transportation system, under the direction of Kenneth Bowden, is
efficient and constantly adapts to our ever expanding needs.
JW
aintenance
<J(itch
en
Annie Mae Fort
Tim Riley Serving
The comprehensive program offered by the Jacksonville Jewish Center
depends on the proper maintenance of -our facilities. The efforts of Tina
Riley and Annie Mae Fort, loyal and devoted members of our maintenance
staff for many years, enable the various activities to take place in pre¬
sentable surroundings. Annie Mae Fort also maintains the Kitchen and
prepares many meals. Additional members of the maintenance staff are
Ralph Coleman, Lilie Mae George and Eugene Davis.
The local caterers, Mr. and Mrs. Albert Meyerhoff, Mrs. Mary Safer,
Mrs. Stanley Ruby and Mrs. Pat Mandell prepare many Simchas for our
members and their families.

The J.F.C.S. is the casework agency of the Jewish
Community. Its main service is counseling on an
individual, family and group basis. A fee, based on
family income, is charged but no one is turned away
because of inability to pay.
It is licensed by the State to provide child
placing services including foster home care, adoptive
placement and work with unmarried mothers.
It provides short term financial assistance to
local families and transients, day camp scholarships
and interest free college loans. Its services to the Aged
includes the local intake for the River Garden Hebrew
Home, services in their own homes and a Friendly
Visiting Program. Its outreach programs consist of a
youth “rap” and encounter group, monthly services
with the patients at the State Hospital at MacClenny,
and a bi-weekly service with the prisoners at Raiford.
In 1972 a total of 368 families or individuals were
served through its various programs. Five children, a
record high for the agency received supervision in
foster homes. On the other hand, because of the lack
of available children for adoption, applications for
adoptions were stopped. Records of inquiries are kept
so that if and when children become available these
people can be contacted. More than $7,000.00 in direct
grants was given to 51 individuals in 38 local families;
about $700.00 for medical and dental costs, about
$500.00 for transient help and almost $300.00 for the
patients at MacClenny and the prisoners at Raiford.
Throughout the years (The J.F.C.S. held its
55th annual meeting last January) the J.F.C.S. sought
to serve the Jewish Community with the latest tech¬
niques and wisdom. Our current terminology stresses
the word “Relevant'’. We seek to be relevant. As part
of our self evaluation, a questionaire was mailed to
the community about needed services. The preliminary
returns confirm our belief that in addition to help
establish two single adult groups (Those over 35 and
those below), there is a need to re-organize the
Friendly Visiting Program. By the time you read this,
we hope to have both these programs going.

River Garden Auxiliary, formerly Ladies Hebrew
Sheltering Aid Society, is entering its 52nd year of
“Service with a Heart”. River Garden Auxiliary was
founded by a group of dedicated women who dream¬
ed of having a home for Jewish aged in Jacksonville.
In 1946 their dream became a reality and the Auxi¬
liary continues to provide a home for the aged giving
them not only shelter but also loving care, companion¬
ship and medical care.
The Auxiliary provides extra budgetary needs as
furnishing, redecorating, air conditioning, kitchen, of¬
fice, and infirmary equipment, and hobby shop mater¬
ials. Funds are raised through our Tombola, Donor,
social printing, candelabra and mezzuzah memorials as
well as dues and life membership. Linen at the Home
is replenished through our annual Linen Shower in
January.
The Auxiliary contributes not only financially to
the Home, but also sponsors activity and volunteer
programs. Golden Sunshine meetings are held weekly.
Officers of River Garden Auxiliary
Our members provide door to door transportation for
non residents to attend these weekly meetings. Resi¬
dents participate in projects served by the Auxiliary
such as ceramic and art classes, hobby shop, and
resident’s magazine. Special parties are planned for
Chanukah, Purim, Mother’s Day, and Father’s Day.
The Red Cross Volunteer Program helps in yet another
way to personalize life for the residents.
As our new year rolls around, we pledge our
continued devotion and service to River Garden and
its residents in the hope that we can help bring some
measure of comfort and meaning to their lives.

The Center’s Thrift Store, Bargain City, has en¬
joyed a very successful year because members j have
helped to donate saleable items to the store located
at 1441 N. Main Street. Several business establish¬
ments have also aided by donating merchandise. Mrs.
Gertrude Slom, manager, is commended for her fine
work and the Center is grateful to the Rummage Com¬
mittee as well, as the store has done a thriving
business.
As we look to the new year we ask your support.
Any items in good condition would be appreciated. Call
the Center and we will be happy to pick up your items.
Our Thrift Shop serves as a fine fund raising pro¬
gram to help offset increased costs of running the
Center and its programs.

The past year was very meaningful. In a way the
Center School was a microcosm of what is occurring
in all Jewish schools. Programs for afternoon schools
are rapidly changing. Day Schools are expanding and
offering many opportunities. In Sunday School, special
short courses were initiated indicating a new trend in
the areas of study.
In Day School, Hebrew studies begin at 8:00 a.m.
for the upper three grades, when pupils are most alert.
Value concepts were stressed. The 25th Anniversary
celebration of the State of Israel proved to be a
source of inspiration for the personal projects and dis¬
plays made by the pupils. Holiday plans and many
synagogue services featured the talents and skills
which were developed. On Pimm they read the Megil-
lah, on Passover they could describe and lead a Seder
by themselves, on Shavuot they chanted the Akdamut
before the reading of the Ten Commandments. These
pupils know why they attend a Jewish religious school,
are proud of it and enjoy the sense of accomplishment
and unity it brings.
A sizeable percentage of our Bar Mitzvah candidates
read the Torah for their own and their friends’ Bar
Mitzvahs. The Hebrew School has been called on for
the reading of Haftorahs on non-Bar Mitzvah Sab¬
baths. In both Hebrew departments several punils
exhibited their individual accomplishments by entering
and scoring well in national contests on knowledge of
Israel and the Bible. Nine of our pupils are attending
Camp Ramah in New England this summer and two
of our graduates are Jr. Counselors in the same camp.
TED GROSS
Principal of Religious School
In the Sunday Religious School, the outstanding
change is noted in the expansion of the mini courses
for the 9th and 10th grades. Courses included: Jewish
Art, Cooking, Modem Israel, Israel Dancing, Jackson¬
ville Jewry, Russian Jewry, the Holocaust, and most
popular, the Yiddish language.
To our committee members, professional and lay
teachers and our secretarial staff, we offer special ex¬
pression of gratitude for helping us achieve the Mish-
naic ideal; the study of Torah excels them all.
Minyan Club: Ted Gross, David Sucholeiki, Scott
Greenfield, Richard Ansbacher, Brian Haft, Nathan
Slaff, Philip Silver, Steven Presser and Sam Rogozinski.
Torah Readers shown with Mr. Gross:
Nathan Gendzier, Steven Presser, Roland Mizrahi, Phi¬
lip Silver, Scott Greenfield.

Who is the Jacksonville Jewish Center? Too
often we talk in abstract terms when describing the
Center and its activities. The fact is that the very
essence of our Synagogue-Cener is the member. That
is why the Year Book is such a treasured publication,
for it records the faces and actions of hundreds of our
friends who throughout the year get involved in the
varied programs of the Center.
Getting involved and doing is “where it’s at” —
the pleasure, the pain, the frustration — but most of
all the pride in seeing accomplishment and progress.
«i# «i» y» «If
A story is 'told about a young boy who once
decided to test the wisdom of the village wise man
and so he approached him with fist closed and asked:
“Wise man, tell me, you who know all, what have I
in my hand?” “You have a bird in your hand”, answer¬
ed the wise man. “How clever you are”, answered the
boy. “Now, tell me is it living or is it dead?”
Awarding of Trophies
at Annual Sports Day
The wise man knew that if he said the bird was
dead, the boy would open his hand and let it fly away.
And if he answered that it was living, he would tighten
his fists and would crush the bird. So he replied
sagely, “as you will it, my boy, as you will it.”
$ * lif. Jj< >|c *
It is our will and our dedication that will deter¬
mine how successful the activities and programs of
the Center will be in the New Year.
“As you will it, as you will it”.
L’Shanah Tovah
A. Robert Gast
Executive Director

===== PAGE 63 =====
ATID is the College Age Organization of Judaism
sponsored by the United Synagogue of America. It
was formed and is led by college students and college-
agers who want to live and learn Judaism and con¬
tinue their identification with the Jewish Movement.
ATID invites all college age students who wish to
involve themselves in Jewish life and thought. ATID
meets on a weekly basis during the summer vacation
to discuss current topics of interest, followed by a
social hour.
These weekly meetings are both inspirational and
enjoyable, and ATID looks forward to seeing more
interested college Jewish youth for the coming sum-
mer.
S.

OÍ4/<
come
We welcome Sandy Kuttler, Youth Studies and
Activities Coordinator.
The Jewish Center Judaic Studies Program for
5734 promises to be a most different one with many
ingredients for an exciting year. A new position of
Youth Studies and Activities Coordinator has been
created to meet this need. Mr. Sanford Kuttler, from
Miami, has been engaged to restructure the nature of
our educational programming.
As Scholar in Residence for the Southeastern
Region of United Synagogue Youth, and in his work
for the National Ramah Commission, Mr. Kuttler
has been involved with programming “Living Judaism”.
Sandy believes strongly that concepts are best acquired
when they are experienced.
The Melton Institute of the Jewish Theological
Seminary stresses the importance of challenging the
basic tenets of Judaism and Mr. Kuttler’s training in
this methodology will lend a new dimension to our
school. “Judaism has remained strong and enduring
due to the questions confronting it; for out of this
confrontation comes better understanding”, he states.
The Teachers’ Institute of the Seminary provided
the additional background that did not come to Mr.
Kuttler after ten years of Hebrew Day Schools. He is
a Baal Korai, Torah Reader, and has studied in depth
the areas of Bible and Tefilah.
Sandy Kuttler places great importance on the
belief that all of life is based on values. The values
accepted by the individual will determine most greatly
the results of his life. He resides at the Gloucester
Apartments and his hobbies include boating, cycling
and Israeli dancing. His duties at the Center wifi
involve directing the U.S.Y. and Kadima activities
as well as the afternoon and Sunday studies program.

MRS. ELEANOR POE

MRS. MILTON LIPKOWITZ
President
B’nai B’rith Women are dedicated to serving you,
your family, your country and humanity. This is car¬
ried through by supporting different national B’nai
B’rith philanthropies such as Bellefaire Children’s
Center; Leo N. Levi Memorial Hospital; National
Jewish Hospital and B’nai B’rith Center for Jewish
patients at Mayo Clinic. B’nai B’rith Women also
sponsors the Children’s Home in Israel and other Is¬
raeli projects. Locally, our activities include: The Anti-
Defamation League (“Dolls for Democracy” program,
distribution of memo calendar books to the public
schools and keeping members aware of the problems
in the Jewish Community); helping at River Garden
Home; sponsoring the B.B. Girls; Hillel at U. of Fla.;
and helping in all community drives. In the coming
year we hope to make the name B’NAI B’RITH syn¬
onymous with our motto: Service, Fidelity and Devo¬
tion.
OUR PROGRESS FO R THE NEW CENTER
WORSHIP. JOY AND GOD LOVE
When I was a child I didn’t mind
To study Hebrew prayer and no other kind.
I went around the world to hunt what is good
So I’ll be a man to appreciate my childhood.
I went here and there — couldn’t get anywhere
But the TORCH OF GOD kept always burning in front of me
everywhere.
I went on the first step to the last step
What a miracle I saw with advance and lots of pep.
Thanks to my parents, teacher and Rabbi,
As I am saved on the day of HOCHAANA-RABBI.
Best Wishes for a Happy New Year
MR. AND MRS. MURRAY GLICKSTEIN
& FAMILY
I start to gather gold sand, and gold spirit
To add another stone to the foundation of the Center
of no limit.
I want a Worship Home
To admire it, rejoice in, as King Solomon’s Throne.
May you please help to add another stone
So your memory will be appreciated for evermore.
GOD will never dwell in a ruined home
AASEY-LEE MICHKAN ABO ELEIKHA

dimension to the environmental and recreational activities in our

In its 21st year, the Mr. and Mrs. Club continues
to enjoy success, achieved through the enthusiasm,
cooperation and imagination of its members, who
conduct the club’s entertainment and general business.
Various monthly affairs are planned by commit¬
tees consisting of married couples, who are dedicated
to presenting an original and outstanding evening of
entertainment for the club members.
An elaborate New Year’s Eve affair is traditional.
Close friendships, sustained for years by many
members’ participation in the club, speak well for the
congeniality of the group and new members are always
welcome. Couples who are interested in joining are
urged to contact the Membership Chairman, Mrs.
Harry Kolchin, 737-8773 or the Treasurer, Mr. Howard
Brown, 724-9200.

Sidney Entman
River Garden Home
OFFICERS & TRUSTEES FOR 1973-74
President Donald D. Cohn
Vice President
Admissions Miss Minnie Schreiber
Co-Chairman Admissions Lewis Meisel
Co-Chairman Admissions Bernard Nachman
Vice President House Committee Eli Becker
Vice President
Residents Welfare Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
Vice President
Public Relations Mrs. Joseph Bernstein
Medical Director Dr. Harry Yoffee
Treasurer Albert Kofka
Secretary Jack Schwager
Investment Counselor Norman Hofheimer
Chairman of the Board Sheldon Gendzier
Other Board members are: Joe Becker, Aaron
Block, Jack Coleman, Abe Diamond, Frank Diamond,
Leon Donziger, Harry Gendzier, Mrs. Gerald Glick-
stein, Mr. and Mrs. William Goldman, S. W. Goldstein,
Arthur J. Gutman, Bennett Hirsh, Mrs. Abe Horovitz,
Sol Katz, Miss Mildred Kaufman, Irving Klepper. Mrs.
Laser Klepper, Martin Lind, Bennie Margol, Rudy
Moss, Mrs. Nathan Newman, Herman Paul, Mrs. My¬
ron Rosenberg, Simon Rothstein, Mrs. Moe Safer, Mrs.
Jack Schwager, Alfred Seitner, Lou Small, David
Stein, Samuel J. Thier, Herbert Warner, Bert Whit¬
field, Edward Witten, Mr. and Mrs. Ike Witten, Mrs.
Paul J. Witten, and Dr. and Mrs. Sam Witten.
Time paused for a moment this year on April 8th
to mark the 27th anniversary of River Garden. Since
1946, the Home has been a haven to the aging and in¬
firm who no longer could manage in the outside world.
Sidney Entman, Executive Director since 1946,
presented his 27th report at the Annual Meeting.
The progression of time has seen many changes
in the scene along the St. Johns River; from an ante¬
bellum style mansion housing 10 people to a three
and a half million dollar complex serving 175 residents
in safety and comfort. In reality, the Home has become
a miniature community with a staff of 135 plus 40
volunteers, a Board of Trustees with 50 members and
over 800 members in the River Garden Auxiliary.
And like a miniature town, the Home is responsible
for delivering all the services needed in any com¬
munity: nursing, counselling, gardening, - and, name
it! - it takes place at River Garden. In reality, the
Home substitutes for the family and neighborhood by
providing a satisfactory life style to the aging and
infirm.
Whom do we serve? We serve the communities of
Florida except the Greater Miami area. Do we serve
the elderly only? There are over 20 residents under
the age of 65 now living in the Home. Do we serve
the long-term patient only? Another quite successful
program is the short-term stay for recuperation and
rehabilitation following time in the hospital when one
is not quite well enough to go home. In 1972 we served
109 patients for 3,795 days care, averaging about 15
days per patient. What responsibility does the Home
have for medical services? In many ways the Home
is a hospital providing similar services excepting surgery
or elaborate diagnostic facilities. The River Garden
resident goes to the hospital only for these special
procedures. Otherwise, the Home keeps all of its resi¬
dents in dignity and allows the final step to take place
in comfort and privacy in their own “home”.
Future objectives of the Home are to continue to
enrich the River Garden communal life by providing
more and more outlets for personal and individual
expression by each resident.

OFFICERS

Gators B.B.G. has had one of its best years ever. From progressive
dinners, slumber parties, Halloween collecting for the underprivileged,
selling New Year’s cards, Operation Chanukah, Surprise Breakfasts, Beach
and Swim parties, car washes, to attending conventions together, with so
much enthusiasm, and having twice as much fun.
Membership doubled as new friendships bloomed. Our love, brother¬
hood, spirit and Jewish identity constantly expands. We are very proud
to have been awarded as the number one chapter of the year in our
council, the International No. 1 programming of the year award for
making the Jewish Servicemen in Viet Nam Chanukah cards, the Council
Program of the Year award, and a Membership Achievement award. We
have also been nominated for the District Program of the year award.
The B.B.Y.O. Spring Convention was hosted by and held in Jacksonville
this year. Along with our brother chapter, A.Z.A., we put loads of planning
and work into this convention. It was one of our council’s best, and was
enjoyed by all who attended it.
There are still many goals which Gators B.B.G. is striving to reach
and many dreams that we wish to accomplish. We welcome interested new
members to step with us into a rewarding new year. Our anxious new
officers are working hard with high hopes for Gators B.B.G.
Bonnie Baumgarten
President

===== PAGE 113 =====
í<Jhe ¡Sanctuaru and hapel
Above all, the Synagogue is a House of Prayer,
a Sanctuary. Here we seek to commune with God, to
expand His presence in our world and derive the inner
strength and inspiration with which to meet life. Here
we celebrate the Sabbath and Holy Days, and dedi¬
cate ourselves to the ideals of Judaism. Here we sanc¬
tify life’s significant occasions.
The Sanctuary, with ceiling seemingly extended to
infinite heights, will be of proportions which neither
dwarf nor magnify Man. Rather, in proper proportions
will he find his true relationship to God and his fellow
men. With reverence and solemn pride will we say,
“How lovely is Thy Tabernacle, O Lord of Hosts!”
The Chapel, a sanctuary in miniature, will provide
an impressive setting for Daily Prayer, small weddings
and will be a fitting House of Study.
OL JZd
vary
an
J 92
esource
Here, indeed, is a spacious room for which we have
a great need. It can be used for dining, concerts or
lectures. Tastefully decorated, it will provide a fitting
setting for “simchas” such as wedding and Bar Mitzvah
receptions. Adjoining the Social Hall will be the Social
Garden where we can enjoy the reception prior to a
dinner. Fully equipped and spacious kitchens will be
provided where dietary laws will be carefully observed.
These complete kitchens will be capable of serving all
types of functions.
To preserve the social and cultural aura of the
Social Hall there will be a Multi-Purpose Auditorium
which will serve many and varied activities. It will be
a gymnasium for athletic and gymnastic activities . . .
an auditorium for both school children and adults . . .
a cafeteria for our Day School and a theater for stage
presentations.
C“Center
Our architects have provided us with a unique but
functional concept of a library. Its unique feature will
be in the manner in which it serves as a learning and
resource center. It is to be an open area in the center
of the school wing which can be converted into an
open and common classroom for the surrounding
classrooms.
A library represents the recorded soul of a people.
Here will be housed the basic written treasures of our
people . . . past . . . present . . and future. Volumes
will be carefully selected to meet the needs of our
school children . . . our adults .. . and our senior adults.
In addition, there will be a special alcove for rare
books; an area for audio-visual education; and display
cases to house historic and antique Judaica items. All
in all, it will be a storehouse from which we will be
able to draw inspiration for the pursuit of greater
intellectual attainments and religious knowledge.

The TIKVA Group of Hadassah meets the sec¬
ond Tuesday of each month from October through
May.
The Summer Book Reviews were enjoyed during
the month of July. Plans are being formulated for a
very stimulating and meaningful year. All members
are invited to participate in our activities, to help meet
our commitments to Israel and to enrich our lives as
American Jewish women.
New members are most welcome.
SHIR LI OFFICERS

The SHIR LI Group of Hadassah was chartered
last year. We hope to grow and add our strength to
the expanding needs of the vital programs of Hadas¬
sah. Mrs. Stanley Gelman (Roz), our founder and
vibrant Membership Vice-President is working dili¬
gently to increase our numbers. Won't you call Roz at

come you.
We are proud of our initial effort in Hadassah and
have many exciting plans for the coming year.
Carol Bettman
Janet Goldstein
• Haschar (Young Judaea) is a national Zionist
Club. Some people have said Haschar is only for
“intelligent people". This isn't always true, but we
are always learning.
This past year in Jacksonville we really didn't
have a senior club. We decided to do something about
it, and built T.U.S.H.Y. (Terrific Union of Sophisti¬
cated Hebrew *Vouth). We don't have many members,
but the Judaeans we have are quite active.
We attended Winter Convention at the Plaza
Hotel in Daytona and Summer Convention at Camp
Judaea in Hendersonville, N.C. At Summer Conven¬
tion we elected our regional Exec and two were from
Jacksonville! Publication Chairman, Carol Bettman,
Va'ad Techun—Janet Goldstein.
Four Judaeans attended our national camp at
Barryville, N.Y., Camp Tel Yehudah. One Judaean was
able to attend the National Convention at Tel Yeh¬
udah, August 23-30.
Meetings are held every Tuesday evening. We
had a lot of help from Hadassah and mostly from our
advisor, Wendy Klein. We hope to continue growing
in Jacksonville and all over the country. Introducing
Judaism, traditions and learning about Israel, is our
main aim.

COMÍ
162
The Center Girl Scout Troop 162 led by Isabel
Balotin and Jackie Lasky offered a very creative and
educational year for all. Starting with division into
The patrols were:
GREEN PEAS
Deborah Bressler, Leader
Susan Epstein, Asst. Leader
Cindy Cohan
Sharon Lasky
Susan Ralph
Troop officers were Susan Epstein and Cindy
Cohan, Treasurer, and Debby Katz, Corresponding
Secretary.
Girl Scout skills with trail signs led to our first
cookout. What fun! After that we had five more cook-
outs which were great successes. Each Scout was taught
camping skills and campout behavior so each would
be prepared for the overnight stay at Camp Chowen-
waw.
Aside from swimming, relays and lots of singing,
it was a year full of creative projects. There was
de’coupage, foil art, ceramics, weaving, horticulture
and most of all good grooming. Whatever project was
undertaken, the Scouts were always enthusiastic.
They donated their time and energy to the Purim
Carnival where they had two very successful booths.
They also took part in the Israeli Cabaret, and
planted trees for Tu B’shvat at the Campsite. The
patrols, each patrol elected a leader who was respon¬
sible for keeping the Scouts in control and making
sure each duty assigned to their patrol was carried out.
BLUE BANANAS
Lori Leff, Leader
Debbie Katz, Asst. Leader
Kara Brown
Kelly Nail
Marcia Greenbaum
Susan Schwab
greatest money maker of all was the annual cookie
sale. With the profits, we all took off to Disney World
for a day, which was truly unforgettable.
Scouting has given the girls an exposure to the
outdoors that many of them could not get otherwise.
And what more perfect place is there than the camp¬
site? Hopefully the girls have learned an awareness
of the wonderful nature around them and how to
cope with it and enjoy it.
It was truly a rewarding year, both for the leaders
and the girls. Whoever said “Youth is wasted on the
young” was never a scout. Special thanks to Linda
Bressler who was the refreshment mother, and Blanche
Slott for her guidance and patience.
Happy Scouting,
Isabel Balotin and Jackie Lasky
RED APPLES
Suzi Lissner, Leader
Judy Bressler, Asst. Leader
Jackie Bloom
Karen Levin
Cathy Levy

COMMITTEE MEMBERS
Members of Tofield Memorial Library Committee
Chairman Minnie Schreiber
Vice Chairman Rita Gendzier
Librarians Mrs. Phillip Selber
Mrs. Bernard Glossman
Friday Evening
Services Mr. & Mrs. Harold Hilsenrad
Publicity and Special Events Mrs. Lewis Meisel
Cataloguer Mrs. Harold Newman
Sisterhood Representative Mrs. Mose Browdy
Administration Mrs. A. Rosenzweig
Mr. Leo Meizlik
Mr. Milton Hecht
Mrs. Irvin B. Rothstein
Mrs. Archie Boatwright
The Tofield Memorial Library has a vast array of
books which have appeal to all members of the congre¬
gation. The library is open every day of the week
through the school office. Members of the committee
man the library on Friday evening after services and
on Sunday mornings.
& . • V.
An Oneg Shabbat sponsored jointly with the Sister¬
hood was a moving event with emphasis on Israel’s
anniversary. A Shalosh Seudot was also sponsored in
May with this same theme.
The congregation is kept abreast of new editions by
publicity in the Bulletin. A very able and dedicated
committee is interested in the library activities.
Library Committee—Seated: Mrs. A. Rosenzweig, Mrs. Mose Browdy, Mrs.
Harold Hilsenrad, Miss Rita Gendzier, Mrs. Philip Selber, Mrs. Irvin B
Rothstein, Miss Minnie Schreiber.
Standing: Mr. Leo Meizlik, Mrs. Bernard Glossman, Mr. Milton Hecht,
Mr. Harold Hilsenrad
(Please note omitted from the picture are Mrs. Lewis Meisel and Mrs.
Harold Newman, who are members of the Library Committee)

nai
B’NAI B’RITH A. B. WEIL LODGE No. 800
President Jack Coleman
President-Elect Ted Freedman
Financial Secretary Hyman Selber
Treasurer Joe P. Safer
Chaplain Rabbi Israel L. Kaplan
Program Chairman Emanuel Ehrlich
Scholarship Fund Chairman Leonard Cohn
and Daniel Hubsch
Publicity Chairman Sam Rosenthal
and Milton Hecht
Hillel Co-Chairman Mayer Becker
Community & Veterans Affairs Chairman Ed Mass
A.D.L. Chairmen Arthur Hurwitz
and Irwin Goldberg
Membership Chairman Hyman Selber
Religious Committee Chairman Jack Becker
Sunshine Committee Chairman Martin Brauner
Once again it is our pleasure to take this oppor¬
tunity to wish one and all a year of health and peace.
We urge all men to join one of the two B’nai B’rith
Lodges to register your support of high ideals so that
we can, through the many arms of B’nai B’rith, help
fight anti-semitics and bigots thereby promoting bene¬
volence and harmony for Jew and non-Jew alike. Please
keep in mind A.D.L. works for you throughout the
world every day.
We wish to bring the community closer together
and it is our intention to have panel programs with
local persons including your Rabbi to participate in the
discussions — “We must hang together or surely we
shall hang separately.”
Once again under the inspiring leadership of Ed
Mass and the philanthropic endeavors of Dr. Louis A.
Wilensky, we were able to present plaques to the
most outstanding teachers of every public school in
the county.
J3 ritli
JACK COLEMAN
President
As usual we were asked by the Salvation Army
to be one of the local service clubs to help them man
their Christmas kettles.
On the International level, B’nai B’rith has over
half a million members in over forty countries, and is
the oldest fraternal organization having been organized
in 1843.
On the national level the Leo N. Levi Hospital in
Hot Springs, Arkansas, for arthritis and kindred
diseases; the National Jewish Hospital in Denver, Colo¬
rado, for tuberculosis and research; the Bellfair Home
in Cleveland, Ohio, for child care services; B’nai B’rith
Center Mayo Clinic in Rochester, Minnesota, for social
service programs; the Franklin D. Roosevelt Four Free¬
dom Library in Washington, D.C., research center for
political science students, and the Salk Foundation for
biological studies are all sponsored by B’nai B’rith.
Come on in, the water is fine!
SAMUEL W. WOLFSON LODGE No. 2588
OFFICERS
President Edward J. Grenadier
1st Vice-President Jack Levine
2nd Vice-President Dennis Wolfson
3rd Vice-President Marshall Fox
Treasurer Stanley Gelman
Financial Secretary Robert Armel
Corresponding Secretary Alfred Freidlin
Recording Secretary Edward Witten
Warden ... Norman I. Brody
Chaplain Dr. Henry Wengrow
The Samuel W. Wolfson Lodge of B’nai B’rith would
like to wish everyone a Happy and Joyous New Year.
We invite all men to join our lodge and the activir
ties in support of the aims of B’nai B’rith, the oldest
and largest Jewish service organization in the world.
B’nai B’rith, and the Wolfson Lodge since its found¬
ing in 1966, has tried always to address itself to the
total spectrum of the Jewish community. Our activities
range from youth (BBYO and Hillel) to leading the
fight against bigotry and prejudice through the Anti-
Defamation League of B’nai B’rith. The support of
EDWARD J. GRENADIER
the State of Israel will always be a prime concern of
B’nai B’rith.
The Wolfson Lodge of B’nai B’rith, with its roots
deep in the oldest monotheistic religion in the world,
strives to keep Judaism an active part of everyday
life. The twin pressures of conformity and complacency
being the antithesis of Judaism, are Judaism’s and
thus B’nai B’rith’s adversaries in the struggle to main¬
tain a unique Jewish identity with its high moral and
ethical standards. We pledge in the coming years, to
continue to fight these evils.

JEWISH RELIGIOUS CALENDAR

PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread — an eight-day celebration of the deliverance of the Jews from
Egyptian slavery. During the traditional Seder meal, on the f irst two nights of Passover, Jewish families read the Hag-
gadah (a ritual recitation of the story of the Exodus). Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according
to special dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzos (unleavened bread) to recall the hasty flight from Egypt,
when there was not time to allow the bread to rise.

===== PAGE 159 =====
Our
High on the list of prescribed duties is the
injunction to make provision for the education of the
young. We will fully discharge this obligation with a
complex of classrooms that will encompass facilities to
handle all facets of education from “early education”,
day school and religious school, to adult studies. Staffed
by a faculty of carefully selected and competent teach¬
ers will be able to offer the best in religious and
'cnooi
secular education.
The physical arrangement of the classrooms em¬
bodies the newest trend. It will consist of a series of
pods. Each grade group will contain three separate
classrooms which can be combined to form one “open
classroom” in which different educational activities can
be carried on simultaneously.
Our
The character development of youth has always
been a prime concern of the Jewish parent. The unrest
among young people today and their search for real
meaning and significance makes the problem more
acute and the solution even more challenging. What
is needed is the possibility of participating in creative
activities of social significance ... of meeting in fellow¬
ship with other Jewish youth for social, emotional and
intellectual experiences.
The Youth Wing, containing a Youth Lounge,
Game Room, Meeting Rooms and Arts and Crafts
facilities will provide the necessary physical accommo¬
dations. Here, under the guidance of trained and
understanding group leaders, our United Synagogue
Youth, Leaders Training Fellowship, Boy Scouts and
our many other youth groups will be able to achieve
new heights in the effectiveness of their programs.

===== PAGE 170 =====
Like a single letter in an alphabet
human life can be meaningless —
or it can be part of a greater meaning.
And we do not want to be meaningless.
How then do we find meaning?
Is it found in things . . .
power, money, the talent of an artist or scientist ?
If so, we will always find someone with
more power, more money, more talent.
Shall we therefore say:
"His life has more meaning than mine."
If we do, we have forgotten the profound
democracy of creation
in which the deepest delights, the most enduring meaning of life
comes from those things all of us are given.
WE HAVE BEEN GIVEN LIFE ITSELF
mind, body, our senses and spirit
the image of God within us.
WE HAVE BEEN GIVEN THE TALENT TO LOVE
the gift of sharing our souls with others
of belonging to them, living on in them.
WE HAVE BEEN GIVEN THE TALENT TO SERVE
the delight which comes in an act of Service itself
without seeking reward.
Consider:
The gift of life: Have you ever discovered
the wonder of the sky?
the rythm of your heart.
Have you seen night pass into day
or a smile change the contours of a face?
The gift of love: Has a child ever slipped
a hand into yours because he wanted to walk with you?
The gift of service: Have you ever found tears in your eyes because
someone clutched your hand and said "thank you"
for an act of consideration?
Why do these things delight us?
It is because they enlarge our life,
They unite us with others
They are endless.
These delights are the birthright and
inheritance of each of us.
They are so close to us we often fail to see them
yet they are given to us freely and we are always free
to reacn for them.
When we do
we find the way to the fullest meaning
in our life.
And to a oneness with God
who endows all with these gifts
which endure forever.
"WE THANK YOU GOD ... FOR THE GIFT OF LIFE"
(From the Prayer Book)
LOUIS PORTNOY - WALTER PORTNOY
AND FAMILIES

PRESIDENT’S MESSAGE
Dear USYers,
The playwrite, George Bernard Shaw, once wrote
that “The worst sin toward our fellow creatures is not
to hate them, but to be indifferent to them.” That, he
said, is the “essence of inhumanity.”
There is a lot of talk today about the new mor¬
ality, over-exposure of the human body on stage and
in movies, pornography on our newsstands, and lower¬
ing of inhibitions regarding our language. While it is
true that some four-letter words are getting very wide
usage today, there is one expression in the English
language that is more obscene than any four-letter
word I know in any language: “So what!!” that is the
really obscene phrase of the ’70s. The person who
doesn’t care for anyone or any cause but himself and
his own welfare, this is the person who is contributing
the most to the ruination of American life.
Only the person who cares, who feels for others,
who accepts his share of the burden, will help save
society. Only the one who agrees to do his share can
look himself in the eye without shame and fear.
The story is told of a jury who returned after two
days of deliberation, and was asked by the judge for
their verdict. “We decided, your honor, not to get in¬
volved.” That is the answer of the typical Ajnerican.
But Judaism teaches us involvement with the
community, caring for our brothers and our fellow hu¬
man beings. To be a Jew means to be involved, to
do something to help suffering humanity. I sincerely
pray that each of you, in 1973, will deepen your com¬
mitment to caring and feeling for those who inhabit
this planet with us. When you do, you will be con¬
tinuing an ancient and hallowed Jewish tradition, first
enunciated by the ancient sage, Hillel: “Separate not
yourself from the community.”
Mark Shorstein
Pres. - Jax., USY
’72-73

WOMEN’S AMERICAN O.R.T.
Organization for Rehabilitation through Training
OFFICERS 1973-74
President Mrs. Myron Dunay (Pepi)
Vice-President Mrs. Alex Rosin (Terri)
Vice-President Mrs. Frank Backilman (Karen)
Vice-President Mrs. Eliot Marcus (Carol)
Vice-President Mrs. George Glossman (Madelyn)
Treasurer Mrs. Charles LeDoyen (Joanne)
Financial Secretary Mrs. Leonard Spillert (Sue)
Recording Secretary Mrs. Nonnan Brody (Carol)
Corresponding Secretary
Mrs. Laurence Resnick (Margie)
Parliamentarian .... Mrs. Joseph Grossberg (Florence)
“Opportunity Reaching Out to Thousands”—This
slogan for the 1973 ORT Day membership drive re¬
presents the ORT effort throughout Israel and the
Diaspora to aid fellow Jews through vocational edu¬
cation and career training. Although education for a
livelihood is the basis of the ORT program, other
factors such as the rise of anti-Semitism, the alienation
of our Jewish youth from their traditions and con¬
tinuous shifts in Jewish population, give further cause
for ORT’s global existance.
Briefly the ORT program consists of:
1. Building and/or maintenance of over 700 voca¬
tional training installations, including schools in Swit¬
zerland and Israel for teacher training.
2. Training more than 70,000 students annually.
3. Providing for the medical, social, recreational
and religious needs of the students.
4. Providing basic and apprenticeship courses as
well as those on highly technical levels.
5. Assisting underdeveloped nations in setting up
vocational training centers.
O.R.T. SCHOOL
In addition to the above, ORT has plans for the
establishment of a pilot school in a Jewish poverty
area here in the United States. With the recent in¬
flation and devaluation of the dollar, the continued in¬
creasing of Israel due to waves of Soviet immigration,
and the plight of many Jews in France, Italy and
Latin America, financing the ORT program is a major
concern of the local chapter. Many fund raising events
are held throughout the year, the largest being our
annual Fall Art Auction. We have also opened a con¬
signment dress shop “Between Us” which we hope
will enable us to meet some of the needs mentioned
above.
Membership is, of course, a vital factor in any
organization, but in ORT this is particularly true. For
every two persons joining ORT another child can be
accepted in an ORT school. The meetings of our day
chapter are usually the second Monday of the month
and in addition to them and our fund raising projects,
we offer study group activities, and new involvement
in American affairs and community service. We also
have a new evening ORT chapter for those who cannot
meet during the day. This chapter usually meets the
third Thursday of each month. Won’t you join us?

National Council of Jewish Women is an organiza¬
tion which, in the spirit of Judaism, is dedicated to
furthering human welfare in the Jewish and general com¬
munities, locally, nationally and internationally. Through
an integrated program of education, service and social ac¬
tion, it provides essential services and stimulates and
educates the individual and the community toward their
responsibilities in advancing, human welfare and the de¬
mocratic way of life.
Our community service projects include such varied
activities as:
1.Mental Health Forum presented each year in March
in conjunction with the Mental Health Association.
Mrs. Leonard Cohn
President
2. Pre-school Vision Screening.
3. Supplying sick-room equipment free to the indigent
of our community.
4. Supervising the sewing of layettes by the residents
of River Garden for distribution by G.J.E.O. to
needy mothers.
5. Tutorial help in 4 Southside Elementary Schools by
our V.I.P. Program.
6. H E.L.P.—working with girls at the Parental Home
in arts and crafts, sewing, cooking etc.

The girls of Brownie Troop #147 enjoyed a
stimulating and exciting year of activities and events.
The year started with a unique decorating pro¬
ject. A room at the Center Campsite was transformed
into a decorator’s dream due to the hard work of the
girls. In addition trips were taken to a Head Start
Center, the Children’s Home, the Animal Shelter and
in every case our girls made things and worked with
other children. Our goal was to make the Brownies
more aware of sharing and giving of themselves to
others.
Other projects included participation in the Scout
Sabbath, Purim Carnival and Scout Round-up. Each
week at our meetings we were involved with arts and
crafts projects, singing, dancing and games. The year
ended with a trip to Disney World.

cWcL7 ions (ELdiication
Sheldon Gendzier
Chairman, Education Comm.
An inspiring teacher, Abraham Joshua Heschel,
once said, “The meaning of existence is found in the
experience of education. Termination of education is the
beginning of despair/' We might add: initiation of edu¬
cation is the beginning of hope.
The same teacher also stated, “The valid test of a
Dr. Saul Eisen
Co-Ch., Education Comm.
student is his ability to ask the right questions." Here
too the logical conclusion is that the valid test of a
teacher is not to give the right answers, but to guide
the student to analyze for himself. On these pages we
present some of the directions taken to fulfill these
precepts.
Ted Gross, Educational Director, and Eleanor Axel- Marilyn Sandler, Day School Principal, and A. Robert
berg, Secretary, reviewing new curriculum materials. Cast, Executive Director, discussing plans for the new
building.

The Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah is a Chap¬
ter on the Group Plan consisting of three groups.
The TIKVA Group is the largest and meets in
the daytime on the second Tuesday of the month
starting in October.
The HANNAH SENESCH Evening Group meets
the second Wednesday evening of the month starting
in September.
The new “Young Matrons” group, SHIR LI, meets
on the second Monday of the month in the evenings.
Each Group is autonomous in its administration
and activities but Chapter functions are planned in¬
volving all the Groups. The Chapter is the co-ordinat¬
ing body and is responsible for all the Groups to the
National and Regional organization.
Standard-setting has always been the hallmark of
Hadassah operations. A veritable “City of Science” —
the Hadassah-Hebrew University Medical Center is
acknowledged to be a peer of the best institutions of
its kind.
The newest “frontier” of the Medical Center com¬
prises the renovated, expanded facilities atop Mount
Scopus, where a School of Occupational Therapy and
a Youth Center are already functioning, and a General
Hospital and Rehabilitation Pavilion are nearing com¬
pletion.
In addition, Hadassah is the principal American
agency supporting the international YOUTH ALIY AH
which helps settle, maintain and educate immigrant
children, and children from disadvantaged and prob¬
lem families.
Hadassah conducts a comprehensive education
program through its HADASSAH ISRAEL EDUCA¬
TION SERVICES. It combines academic studies with
fundamental vocational training. Hadassah has estab¬
lished one of the most advanced educational concepts
— the two year Community College.
HASHACHAR, the largest Zionist Youth Move¬
ment in the United States is sponsored by the Hadas¬
sah Zionist Youth Commission. The Hashachar program
consist of the Young Judaean Clubs, College Campus
Programs, Camping Programs and Israel Programs.
Hadassah continues to meet its long and firm
commitment to the JEWISH NATIONAL FUND.
Hadassah is a non-governmental organization of
the United Nations. It is also affiliated nationally with
organizations whose goals it approves, among them:
the World Health Organization, Foreign Policy Assn,
and the American Council of Voluntary Agencies.
Newcomers to Jacksonville and non-members are
invited to join one of the groups and help carry on the
work that was started over sixty years ago and has
grown with the birth and rebuilding of Israel and
with Jewish life in the United States.

Their stimulating cultural, educational and fund-raising

further development and security of Israel. Meetings
are held the second Wednesday of each month in Mt.

The Jewish Community Council is the social plan¬
ning, community relations and fund raising body serv¬
ing the entire Jewish community of Jacksonville and
its environs. Its representative Board of Dirctors is
composed of delegates of all the major Jewish religious,
Philanthropic, and service organizations in the area,
plus members-at-large.
The Council's Women's Division sponsors a separate
drive for the women in the community on behalf of
the United Jewish Welfare Fund.
The annual community-wide United Jewish Welfare
Fund Campaign provides funds for the support of local,
national and overseas needs, including the United
Jewish Appeal, and the Israel Emergency Fund.
Local beneficiaries receiving funds are River Garden
Hebrew Home for the Aged; the Jewish Family &
Children's Services. The Council is the sponsoring
agency for Mount Carmel Gardens.
The Community Calendar and its clearance service
is maintained by the Jewish Community Council to
enable all Jewish organizations in the community to
clear dates for all their events.
The Council publishes the COMMENTATORy a
newspaper published monthly. It is the official paper
of the Council and its member organizations.
The Council conducts community programs in cele¬
bration of special events and, through its Youth Divi¬
sion, promotes various programs for the Jewish youth
of all ages. The Council also sponsors two softball
teams in the City Recreation League.
The Council is a member of the Council of Jewish
Federations and Welfare Funds, the National Jewish
Community Relations Advisory Council ad the Com¬
munity Planning Council of the Jacksonville area.

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily, morn¬
ing and evening in the Chapel. Friday Family Services, Sabbath and
Holiday Services are observed in the Sanctuary throughout the entire
year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs are
observed in connection with Circumcision, Naming of Girls, Pidyon
Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat*Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages, Conver¬
sion to Judaism, Bereavement.
Educatior — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with outstanding pro¬
gram of secular and religious content. Hebrew School, carefully graded
with intensive Hebrew content; Sunday School, with a curriculum in¬
cluding Jewish history and customs; Solomon Schechter Day School,
a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st through 6th grade; with Pre-
Jr., Junior, Teen and Confirmation Congregations and Minyan Clubs.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of juvenile and
adult Jewish books; Adult Education, religious and cultural Jewish
courses; Music programs of religious and cultural Jewish music; Sis¬
terhood and Young Men’s Clubs; Social activities provided by Mr.
and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation Program (Olympic
Pool), summer weekdays and Sundays for all ages at our Campsite.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United Synagogue
Youth, various age levels are served by Center Youth League, Charles
Palot Club, Kadima Club; Scouting (Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts); Girl
Scouts; Brownies; Athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center
Day Camp & nine weeks summer program at our 36 acre Campsite on
State Road No. 13.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . Swimming,
athletics, snack bar, available for members during the summer months.
KENNETH BOWDEN
FLORENCE ROBIN
ESTHER FISHLER

DAVID GOODMAN,
President
The Zionist Organization of America is unquestion¬
ably the outstanding American Jewish organization
whose purpose is exclusively for Israel. It is a political
group (not a fund raising), designed to oppose pres¬
sure on the U.S. government by oil interests, etc. These
business interests, aided by other pro-Arab groups, con¬
stantly seek to force our government into a pro-Arab
policy.
The Z.O.A. is the only organization which can ef¬
fectively counter anti-Israel political pressure in Wash¬
ington. It behooves every American Jew to join the
Z.O.A., just as we must support Israel in other ways.
In addition to its main purpose, the Z.O.A. provides
Dinner in Honor of Mr. David Moss
From left to right: Rabbi Scheindlin, Emanuel Ehrlich,
Cantor Marton, Mr. & Mrs. David Moss, Jack Becker
an excellent magazine for its members, supports the
Mollie Goodman academic high school, and the KFar
Silver (for American Jewish youth) in Israel, and pro¬
vides a home for visiting Americans in Tel Aviv with
the Z.O.A. House there.
It is imperative that in these times, when the Nixon
administration is being so strongly influenced by the
oil interests with whom it has a very close personal
relationship, that every American Jew should put his
name on record in support of Israel by joining Z.O.A.
Membership is $20 per year. Stand up and be counted
by calling David Goodman, Bruno Loehner or Albert
Meyerhoff.
Dr. Bernard Marks, David Goodman and Rabbi David
Gaffney at a Z.O.A. Meeting.
183
===== PAGE 218 =====
enter
The Center Day Camp, founded in 1946 by the
Jacksonville Jewish Center, has continued its growth
each year and broadened its scope of activities to in¬
clude the finest programming and facilities of any
Day Camp in this area.
With emphasis on a qualified professional staff,
many diversified activities are made available to camp¬
ers between the ages of 3 to 15 years. The camp sea¬
son included three three-week sessions. Each session
incorporated Judaic awareness and Judaic living
through nature, play and sports. Every session em¬
phasized a particular phase of our excellent water
sports and athletic program, plus interesting trips away
from camp.
Arts and crafts projects were designed to help
each child develop his own creative ability. Some of
the most popular crafts were fruit baskets and candy
dishes made from phonograph records, Mexican wall
hangings, wind chimes and vases cut from glass bot¬
tles. Emphasis was placed on natural materials found
on the site such as bamboo, pine cones, branches,
leaves, rocks, etc.
The Camp continues to offer an excellent swim¬
ming program where a camper may advance from be¬
ginners through Junior Life Saving. The program also
included free swim periods each afternoon.
•
Facilities include many acres of wooded area for
primitive camping and nature trails, an Olympic swim¬
ming pool, a well-equipped playground, softball dia¬
mond, basketball court and areas for all active sports.
Blanche Slott
Camp Director
Presentation of American Flag by
Mr. Max Becker, representing"Post 199,
Jewish War Veterans of U.S.
Bogrot — 9-10 Yr. Campers with
Counselor Alison Brown and CIT’s Beth Meisel
and Karen Mannis

Cantor Abraham Marton has been our Hazzan for
more than twenty-five years. He is the director of the
Choral Society which is also the Choir for Friday eve¬
ning Services and the High Holy Days. He also directs
the Glee Club which is composed of students in our
school.
Cantor Isaac Goodfriend of Atlanta, Ga. and Cantor
Louis Danto of Cleveland, Ohio, who were the soloists
at the Annual Concert.
Cantor A. Marton
This year Cantor Marton
presented an outstanding Con¬
cert at the Civic Auditorium
Little Theatre.
Besides the guest soloists,
both the Choir and Glee Club
presented a variety of beauti¬
ful numbers and had several
soloists of their own.
In addition, another Con¬
cert was given at the Mt. Car¬
mel Apartments Auditorium.
Combined Glee Club and Choir performing at the Annual Concert
at the Civic Auditorium.

Dear Friends:
One of the effects which the coming High Holi¬
days have on the mind of the Jew is an acute aware¬
ness of the passing of time. We look back and
comment upon how quickly the old year has sped by
and we contemplate with some concern the year
which lies ahead. In our prayers we ask to be inscribed
in the Book of Life. I pray that the New Year bring
health, happiness and peace to us and to all of
mankind.
We are brought together as a community by the
primary institution of Jewish life and affiliation. Each
of us may be drawn to the Jewish enterprise by dif¬
ferent chords which play on our life. For some it is a
fond memory of the past. For others, it is the desire to
secure a Jewish future for our children. Others are
moved by noble sentiment of community and phil¬
anthropic service and others respond to the Jewish
core within themselves, when they feel threatened by
those they see as the enemies of their people. What¬
ever the impulse, experience has shown that without
a Center, the touchstone of Jewish life which is the
synagogue, all of the activities of Jewish life are
doomed to failure. The synagogue is the source, the
rich earth which provides the nutrients with which
the roots and branches are fed; the heart which
pumps the life sustaining fluid to the necessary but
tangential limbs which make up Jewish life.
We come to the synagogue to probe our weakness
and strength, to consider the gap between what we
practice and what we profess. We come to the syna¬
gogue to quiet the turbulence of our heart; to engage
in self renewal and regeneration of our sensitivities
and values and to remain conscious of the source of
all being which we call God. We are each enriched by
our affiliation to the core of our collective life, the
synagogue, and impoverished in its neglect.
RABBI DAVID GAFFNEY
We are on the threshold of taking a bold and
decisive step in the future life of our comumnity. All
of our efforts in the very near future will be to build a
structure with which to house our Torah, to witness
our prayers and to serve as a center in which we and
our children will be guided and instructed in Jewish
life and fellowship. No doubt we will succeed in these
efforts. Whether we succeed in providing this structure
with a soul depends on an effort of a different sort.
May we succeed in nurturing the vision and
dream of Jacob; the vision of an earth joined to
heaven, of a society distinguished by the quality of
compassion and humanity, of a people true to their
selves and to God.
llalli j£)aviJ Cjaffney

Cub Pack 14
Cubmaster . Sidney Engelhardt
Den Mother Coach Mrs. David Mizrahi
Committee Chairman Dr. David Mizrahi
Assistant Chairman Gerald Greenspoon
Den Leaders Webelos - Gene Crystal
Den I - Marlene Bossen, Toby Ringel
Den II - Clara Mann, Gilda Yeston
Den III - Carol Schneider, Anita Garfinkel,
Susie Cherry
Cub Scouting, as an introduction to the Boy
Scouts, continues to be one of the outstanding youth
programs offered at the Center. Boys between the
ages of 8 and 10 find many new and challenging
experiences in Cubbing.
The Cubs in each Den become a close unit as
they work together to develop new hobbies, take field
trips, explore nature and work on craft projects and
skits. The annual Rocket and Pinewood Derbies give
Gene Crystal, Clara Mann, Susie Cherry,
Carol Schneider, Marlene Bossen, Toby Ringel,
Judy Mizrahi and Sidney Engelhardt
the boys the opportunity to design their own rockets
and cars and enter them in competitive racing.
In honor of Boy Scout Week, the Cubs partici¬
pate in the late Friday Sabbath Service and the
annual Blue and Gold Banquet. To close a most
successful year, all members of Pack 14 and their
families gathered at the Center Southside Site for
an evening of swimming, sports, games and cooking
over an open fire. The highlight of the event was
the presentation of the Larry Hacker Cub Scout
Award to the Cub scoring the highest in attendance at
Den and Pack Meetings, achievement and good will.
The recipient for 1973 was Gary Kupfer. An individual
plaque was presented to Gary and two perpetual
plaques will be on display at the Center.
The Shofar Award to recognize outstanding ser¬
vice by adults in the promotion of Scouting among
Jewish boys was presented to Commander Sidney
Engelhardt, Cubmaster of Pack 14, in recognition of
sixteen years devoted service to the Cub scout program.
Awards Presentation by Sidney Engelhardt
to members of the Pack

===== PAGE 265 =====
President’s Message
As we approach the New Year, we express our
gratitude and appreciation to the many members of
our Congregation who have so generously and willingly
cooperated and worked with the leadership of the
Center during the past year. As a result of our com¬
bined efforts, we have developed a record of which
we can all be proud.
While reviewing the past, it is fitting to recall the
history of our Center which dates back to the founding
of Congregation B’nai Israel in 1901. At the time of
its incorporation in 1903, the membership had grown
from 5 to 40 families. The first building was acquired
several years later, at a time when the membership
reached 75 families. One of the highlights of the past
year was obtaining the cornerstone and a Nabisco tin
can contained therein which included a letter from
the Building Committee of Congregation B’nai Israel,
dated September 6, 1908. We are attempting to pre¬
serve the contents of that tin can and place them in
an appropriate place in our new building.
In 1927, Congregation B’nai Israel became the
Jacksonville Jewish Center. The real history of the
Center is seen in the panorama of generations and
events over more than seventy years. In wartime and
peacetime, in depression and in prosperity, in sorrow
and in great joy, a dynamic and forth-right religious
response to the needs of the Jewish people has been
the purpose and the achievement of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center.
Now it is our generation that is making history.
We acquired the beautiful new site in November 1963
for our new Synagogue Center Complex and after
almost ten years of planning and working we scheduled
the ground breaking ceremony for September 16, 1973.
As our dream approaches reality, there is much
still to be done. Each of us must contribute no less
than his or her earnest share so that we can build from
strength to strength. We must join together and leave
our footprints in the halls of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center for future generations to remember. Though
the task is great, the reward is far greater.
On behalf of the officers and directors, may we
extend each of you a year of good health and happiness
and together may we move closer toward the com¬
pletion of our new home.
Sincerely,
Jack F. Shorstein
President
WILBUR MARGOL
Chairman of the Board

Audience listening intently as Mr. Ashman reveals some
interesting facts about Henry Kissinger
The Young Men’s Club of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center enjoyed a year of varied and successful acti¬
vities. The Club was led by an Executive Committee
composed of Martin Edwards, Larry Figur, Alan Gor¬
don, Meyer Leibo, Dr. H. Ronald Levin, Dr. Harvey
Pearl, Ronald Pollock and Barry Sinoff.
The objectives of the Young Men’s Club are:
a. To serve as a forum for the presentation of
contemporary issues as well as social, athletic
and cultural activities.
b. To provide service to the congregation, and
community, when needed.
c. To broaden the perspectives and deepen the
feelings of Jewishness of the members.
One of the highlights of the year occurred on
Sunday, April 29 when Charles Ashman, noted author
and broadcaster, spoke to a large gathering. Ashman,
author of Kissinger: The Adventurers of Super-Kraut
was stimulating, entertaining and provocative. Every¬
one was in agreement that it would be a long time
before the enjoyment of the evening would be dupli¬
cated.
Other events sponsored by the Club included:
A Dinner-Discussion with Tom Wasdin, Basketball
Coach of Jacksonville University, a celebrity cocktail
hour and reception for husbands and wives prior to
the Ashman program, co-sponsoring the Athletic
Awards Ceremony for participants in the Basketball
Clinic, and sponsoring a Basketball Team in the City
Church League.
Charles Ashman and Sybil Ansbacher Athletic awards
at the Celebrity Reception ceremony at
the Center Campsite
Jack F. Shorstein, Charles Ashman
and Stanley Kantor reminiscing
about their experiences at the
University of Florida 4!
